guiding_principles_for_20_years_of_programming

Posted on 2020-03-08
  1. Donâ€™t fight the tools: libraries, language, platform, etc. Use as much native constructs as possible. Donâ€™t bend the technology, but donâ€™t bend the problem either. Pick the right tool for the job or youâ€™ll have to find the right job for the tool you got.
  2. You donâ€™t write the code for the machines, you write it for your colleagues and your future self (unless itâ€™s a throw away project or youâ€™re writing assembly). Write it for the junior ones as a reference.
  3. Any significant and rewarding piece of software is the result of collaboration. Communicate effectively and collaborate openly. Trust others and earn their trust. Respect people more than code. Lead by example. Convert your followers to leaders.
  4. Divide and conquer. Write isolated modules with separate concerns which are loosely coupled. Test each part separately and together. Keep the tests close to reality but test the edge cases too.
  5. Deprecate yourself. Donâ€™t be the go-to person for the code. Optimize it for people to find their way fixing bugs and adding features to the code. Free yourself to move on to the next project/company. Donâ€™t own the code or youâ€™ll never grow beyond that.
  6. Security comes in layers: each layer needs to be assessed individually but also in relation to the whole. Risk is a business decision and has direct relation to vulnerability and probability. Each product/organization has a different risk appetite (the risk they are willing to take for a bigger win). Often these 3 concerns fight with each other: UX, Security, Performance.
  7. Realize that every code has a life cycle and will die. Sometimes it dies in its infancy before seeing the light of production. Be OK with letting go. Know the difference between 3 categories of features:
    Core: like an engine in a car. The product is meaningless without it.
    Necessary: like a carâ€™s spare will. Itâ€™s rarely used but when needed, its function decides the success of the system.
    Added value: like a carâ€™s cup-holder. Itâ€™s nice to have but the product is perfectly usable without it.
  8. Donâ€™t attach your identity to your code. Donâ€™t attach anyoneâ€™s identity to their code. Realize that people are separate from the artifacts they produce. Donâ€™t take code criticism personally but be very careful when criticizing othersâ€™ code.
  9. Tech debt is like fast food. Occasionally itâ€™s acceptable but if you get used to it, itâ€™ll kill the product faster than you think (and in a painful way).
  10. When making decisions about the solution all things equal, go for this priority:
    Security > Usability (Accessibility & UX) > Maintainability > Simplicity (Developer experience/DX) > Brevity (code length) > *Performance
    *    But donâ€™t follow that blindly because it is dependent on the nature of the product. Like any career, the more experience you earn, the more you can find the right balance for each given situation. For example, when designing a game engine, performance has the highest priority, but when creating a banking app, security is the most important factor.
  11. Bugsâ€™ genitals are called copy & paste. Thatâ€™s how they reproduce. Always read what you copy, always audit what you import. Bugs take shelter in complexity. â€œMagicâ€ is fine in my dependency but not in my code.
  12. Donâ€™t only write code for the happy scenario. Write good errors that answer why it happened, how it was detected and what can be done to resolve it. Validate all system input (including user input): fail early but recover from errors whenever possible. Assume the user hold a gun: put enough effort into your errors to convince them to shoot something other than your head!
  13. Donâ€™t use dependencies unless the cost of importing, maintaining, dealing with their edge cases/bugs and refactoring when they donâ€™t satisfy the needs is significantly less than the code that you own.
  14. Stay clear from hype-driven development. But learn all you can. Always have pet projects.
  15. Get out of your comfort zone. Learn every day. Teach what you learn. If youâ€™re the master, youâ€™re not learning. Expose yourself to other languages, technologies, culture and stay curious.
  16. Good code doesnâ€™t need documentation, great code is well documented so that anyone who hasnâ€™t been part of the evolution, trial & error process and requirements that led to the current status can be productive with it. An undocumented feature is a non-existing feature. A non-existing feature shouldnâ€™t have code.
  17. Avoid overriding, inheritance and implicit smartness as much as possible. Write pure functions. They are easier to test and reason about. Any function thatâ€™s not pure should be a class. Any code construct that has a different function, should have a different name.
  18. Never start coding (making a solution) unless you fully understand the problem. Itâ€™s very normal to spend more time listening and reading than typing code. Understand the domain before starting to code. A problem is like a maze. You need to progressively go through the code-test-improve cycle and explore the problem space till you reach the end.
  19. Donâ€™t solve a problem that doesnâ€™t exist. Donâ€™t do speculative programming. Only make the code extensible if it is a validated assumption that itâ€™ll be extended. Chances are by the time it gets extended, the problem definition looks different from when you wrote the code.
  20. Software is more fun when itâ€™s made together. Build a sustainable community. Listen. Inspire. Learn. Share.

20_Things_to_know_before_you_start_programming

Posted on 2020-03-07

Everyone who started coding started out pretty much in the same seat as you. It can be extremely frustrating. A lot of people tend to give up when they start teaching themselves to code. It can be overwhelming, and not bearable at times. Thatâ€™s why the CTO and Co-found of TheFireHouseProject.com were nice enough to tell us 20 things to know before you start programming.

Here are the top things to know before you start programming:

  • You learn by doing
  • Programming is not like studying for a test
  • Cheating is very well acceptable
  • Letting problems pile up is a poor strategy
  • You do not need 5 monitors
  • Pair programming as much as possible
  • Thereâ€™s a right way to ask for help
  • Always celebrate small wins
  • Never stop learning

What subject do students like a lot?

Memorizing does not apply on programming

You learn by doing is on the first place of â€œ20 Things to know before you start programmingâ€ list for a reason. The only way to get better at programming is to actually code. Do not let paralysis prevent you from starting. Ever.

Programming is not like studying for a test by a mile. When you study, you memorize things in order to get a better grade. This does not apply to programming, at all. It doesnâ€™t matter at all if you forget how to do a switch case, or an if/else statement for that matter. As long as you know that something does that, youâ€™re golden.

Cheating is completely acceptable. Hell, itâ€™s even encouraged. The top programmers Iâ€™ve had to deal with use Google on a regular basis. This way, youâ€™ll save tremendously much time on trying to fix something yourself. This doesnâ€™t mean that you shouldnâ€™t write any piece of code on yourself. But, if youâ€™re stuck Google is your friend.

The top programming languages of 2019

Letting problems pile up is the poorest strategy there is. I used to make countless changes on my code and expected it to work right away with no errors. The problem with this path is that it stacks problems one over another. It will become difficult to deal with them because you will not know where the problem is. So take them one by one.

Learning by yourself is very tough. The majority of people started off programming by trying to learn by themselves. Itâ€™s not that bad of a choice, but itâ€™s very time-consuming. I remember when I didnâ€™t know how the map function worked on JavaScript. I spent all weekend trying to figure it out, and I didnâ€™t get very far. Then, on Monday a friend of mine explained it for 5 minutes and I got it completely.

Your ego doesnâ€™t have a place while youâ€™re learning to code

Let go of your emotions. Youâ€™re going to see errors on your journey to programming a lot. A lot lot. Youâ€™ll even get familiarized with them. When screwing up is such a big part of the process, you have to be okay with the mistakes you made. Donâ€™t hold grudges, as youâ€™ll only lose time and patience.

You donâ€™t need 5 monitors. Canâ€™t stress that enough. Despite what Hollywood shows on movies, you never need an insane external monitor to be a programmer. The computer youâ€™re on right now is probably good enough for you to start programming. Donâ€™t waste money on something that might not be useful. Iâ€™ve been using 2 monitors for the past 15 years and Iâ€™m pretty okay.

Capitalization matters. It took me a while to understand this. Thereâ€™s a subtle difference between similar symbols, and it can be pretty frustrating until you get used to it. I remember pointing out to the computer â€œHey, hereâ€™s the variable I declared. Are you blind?â€

SDK usage shows the most popular programming language

Trying to understand everything is a lost cause. In the beginning, youâ€™re full of energy. You chase down the â€œwhyâ€ of every single problem you encounter. This is not necessary by a mile. Computers are so complex and there is a sea full of things to learn. And youâ€™re never going to know everything. And thatâ€™s really okay.

Pair the program as much as possible. Thereâ€™s no faster way until to this day to learn how to code. Pair program as much as you can, and youâ€™ll be amazed at the results.

Ask for help without feeling embarrassed

Changing code is a huge part of the process. I used to think back in my young days that every piece of code I wrote had to be perfect. But making improvements to your code is normal. Hell, itâ€™s essential. Youâ€™re not writing a book that canâ€™t be changed once itâ€™s published. So if you feel like your code might run faster another way, do it.

Thereâ€™s a right way to ask for help. Everybody has to ask for help at some point in their career. And when you do, remember these four things:

  1. Overcommunicate details that you see
  2. Explain just whatâ€™s happening, nothing more
  3. Explain what should be happening
  4. State why you think it has to be working differently

Go through this process, and youâ€™ll often come across a solution without having to ask for help. It makes you think about your problem in a holistic way.

You donâ€™t have to be a math genius. If youâ€™re not a math person, or into math at all, it doesnâ€™t mean that you wonâ€™t succeed in programming. Itâ€™s a huge advantage if you do, but if not, itâ€™s not an indicator that you will fail programming. I know someone who doesnâ€™t know how to multiply numbers without a calculator and he excels in Python.

Always celebrate small wins. Building stuff with code is extremely cool. I wouldâ€™ve never arrived where Iâ€™m at if I hadnâ€™t celebrated every win. Itâ€™s amazing to see how well your code runs once you optimize it. And itâ€™s a very powerful tool to pat yourself in the back if no one is doing it for you.

Pair programming is similar to having a navigator while you drive

Meet-ups are extremely valuable. In the beginning, I found it pretty intimidating to get myself to the point where I found myself comfortable enough to attend these. But once I did, I realized that there were a lot of programmers just like me. And you will be amazed by how many people are going through the same things as you.

Itâ€™s okay to make big mistakes

Avoid merge conflicts and youâ€™ll be happy. Merge conflicts are probably the most annoying thing that can happen. Especially on a Friday. When I realized that I could sneak a feature in before a teammate so that he has to deal with it instead of me, I was super excited. This isnâ€™t the most morally advise I give, but it helps.

Itâ€™s okay to admit what you donâ€™t know. When you land your first programming job, you might stick to the â€œfake it till you make itâ€ agenda. Donâ€™t. Nobody expects you to know everything right away. And the truth will get you farther than a lie ever will. Donâ€™t expect people to believe you know everything on your first job. Having the ability to know itâ€™s okay is crucial to the â€œ20 Things to know before you start programmingâ€ list.

It doesnâ€™t take 10,000 hours to land a good job. Thereâ€™s a saying â€œyou need 10,000 hours of work to be good at somethingâ€. This doesnâ€™t apply to programming at all. In reality, you need to be good enough to know how to self-correct and get back on track when some problem arises. And, this goes without saying, that it takes less than 10,000 hours.

Youâ€™re going to wake up thinking about code. And when it happens, itâ€™s amazing. Youâ€™ll find out that you love what youâ€™re doing. In fact, youâ€™re loving it so much that itâ€™s the first thing you think about when you wake up.

Itâ€™s okay to make big mistakes. I once made a mistake so big that it cost my company 10,000. And in the process, I realized the most important lesson of my programming language: itâ€™s okay to make mistakes. Through this mistake, I realized what I shouldnâ€™t do. Ever. And I passed this lesson further below and we never had to pay â€‹10,000 again.

Big mistakes can lead to a more sustainable future

Concurrency_vs_Parallelism

Posted on 2020-03-05 Edited on 2020-03-06

Concurrency means multiple tasks which start, run, and complete in overlapping time periods, in no specific order. Parallelism is when multiple tasks OR several part of a unique task literally run at the same time, e.g. on a multi-core processor. Remember that Concurrency and parallelism are NOT the same thing.

Letâ€™s understand more in detail that what I mean when I say Concurrency vs. Parallelism.

Concurrency

Concurrency is essentially applicable when we talk about minimum two tasks or more. When an application is capable of executing two tasks virtually at same time, we call it concurrent application. Though here tasks run looks like simultaneously, but essentially they MAY not. They take advantage of CPU time-slicing feature of operating system where each task run part of its task and then go to waiting state. When first task is in waiting state, CPU is assigned to second task to complete itâ€™s part of task.

Operating system based on priority of tasks, thus, assigns CPU and other computing resources e.g. memory; turn by turn to all tasks and give them chance to complete. To end user, it seems that all tasks are running in parallel. This is called concurrency.

Parallelism

Parallelism does not require two tasks to exist. It literally physically run parts of tasks OR multiple tasks, at the same time using multi-core infrastructure of CPU, by assigning one core to each task or sub-task.

Parallelism requires hardware with multiple processing units, essentially. In single core CPU, you may get concurrency but NOT parallelism.

Differences between concurrency vs. parallelism

Now letâ€™s list down remarkable differences between concurrency and parallelism.

Concurrency is when two tasks can start, run, and complete in overlapping time periods. Parallelism is when tasks literally run at the same time, eg. on a multi-core processor.

Concurrency is the composition of independently executing processes, while parallelism is the simultaneous execution of (possibly related) computations.

Concurrency is about dealing with lots of things at once. Parallelism is about doing lots of things at once.

An application can be concurrent â€“ but not parallel, which means that it processes more than one task at the same time, but no two tasks are executing at same time instant.

An application can be parallel â€“ but not concurrent, which means that it processes multiple sub-tasks of a task in multi-core CPU at same time.

An application can be neither parallel â€“ nor concurrent, which means that it processes all tasks one at a time, sequentially.

An application can be both parallel â€“ and concurrent, which means that it processes multiple tasks concurrently in multi-core CPU at same time .

Thatâ€™s all about Concurrency vs. Parallelism, a very important concept in java multi-threading concepts.
